<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Longest river in Asia</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Long river", "Yangzi", and "Changjiang" redirect here. For a list of rivers by length, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_rivers_by_length" title="List of rivers by length">List of rivers by length</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Long_River_(disambiguation)" title="Long River (disambiguation)">Long River (disambiguation)</a>, <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Yangzi_(disambiguation)" title="Yangzi (disambiguation)">Yangzi (disambiguation)</a>, and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Changjiang_(disambiguation)" title="Changjiang (disambiguation)">Changjiang (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Golden_Island,_on_The_Yang-Tse_River,_China_(LMS,_1869,_p.64).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="774" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Golden_Island%2C_on_The_Yang-Tse_River%2C_China_%28LMS%2C_1869%2C_p.64%29.jpg/220px-Golden_Island%2C_on_The_Yang-Tse_River%2C_China_%28LMS%2C_1869%2C_p.64%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Golden_Island%2C_on_The_Yang-Tse_River%2C_China_%28LMS%2C_1869%2C_p.64%29.jpg/330px-Golden_Island%2C_on_The_Yang-Tse_River%2C_China_%28LMS%2C_1869%2C_p.64%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Golden_Island%2C_on_The_Yang-Tse_River%2C_China_%28LMS%2C_1869%2C_p.64%29.jpg/440px-Golden_Island%2C_on_The_Yang-Tse_River%2C_China_%28LMS%2C_1869%2C_p.64%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Golden_Island,_on_The_Yang-Tse_River,_China_(LMS,_1869,_p.64).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jinshan_Temple_(Zhenjiang)" title="Jinshan Temple (Zhenjiang)">Golden Island</a>, on The Yang-Tse River, China (<a href="/wiki/London_Missionary_Society" title="London Missionary Society">LMS</a>, 1869, p.64)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fruits-LMS_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fruits-LMS-7">[7]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Qutang_Gorge_along_the_Yangtze_river.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/The_Qutang_Gorge_along_the_Yangtze_river.JPG/220px-The_Qutang_Gorge_along_the_Yangtze_river.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/The_Qutang_Gorge_along_the_Yangtze_river.JPG/330px-The_Qutang_Gorge_along_the_Yangtze_river.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/The_Qutang_Gorge_along_the_Yangtze_river.JPG/440px-The_Qutang_Gorge_along_the_Yangtze_river.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Qutang_Gorge_along_the_Yangtze_river.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Qutang_Gorge" title="Qutang Gorge">Qutang Gorge</a> along the Yangtze River.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1768" data-file-width="2847" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg/220px-ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg/330px-ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg/440px-ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Three_Gorges_Dam" title="Three Gorges Dam">Three Gorges Dam</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hubei" title="Hubei">Hubei</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tiger_Leaping_Gorge.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Tiger_Leaping_Gorge.jpg/220px-Tiger_Leaping_Gorge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Tiger_Leaping_Gorge.jpg/330px-Tiger_Leaping_Gorge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Tiger_Leaping_Gorge.jpg/440px-Tiger_Leaping_Gorge.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tiger_Leaping_Gorge.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tiger_Leaping_Gorge" title="Tiger Leaping Gorge">Tiger Leaping Gorge</a> in <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>Yangtze</b> or <b>Yangzi</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><small>English:  </small><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/j/: 'y' in 'yes'">j</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="/ŋ/: 'ng' in 'sing'">ŋ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span> or <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/j/: 'y' in 'yes'">j</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="/ŋ/: 'ng' in 'sing'">ŋ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans">长江</span>; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">長江</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Cháng Jiāng</span></i>; <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a></small> 'long river'), is the longest <a href="/wiki/List_of_rivers_of_Asia" title="List of rivers of Asia">river in Asia</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_rivers_by_length" title="List of rivers by length">third-longest</a> in the world, and the longest in the world to flow entirely within one country. It rises at Jari Hill in the <a href="/wiki/Tanggula_Mountains" title="Tanggula Mountains">Tanggula Mountains</a> (Tibetan Plateau) and flows 6,300 km (3,900 mi) in a generally easterly direction to the <a href="/wiki/East_China_Sea" title="East China Sea">East China Sea</a>. It is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_rivers_by_discharge" title="List of rivers by discharge">seventh-largest river by discharge volume in the world</a>. Its <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">drainage basin</a> comprises one-fifth of the land area of China, and is home to nearly one-third of the <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_China" title="Demographics of China">country's population</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Yangtze has played a major role in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_China" title="History of China">history</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Culture_of_China" title="Culture of China">culture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_China" title="Economy of China">economy of China</a>. For thousands of years, the river has been used for water, irrigation, sanitation, transportation, industry, boundary-marking, and war. The prosperous <a href="/wiki/Yangtze_Delta" title="Yangtze Delta">Yangtze Delta</a> generates as much as 20% of <a href="/wiki/Historical_GDP_of_China" title="Historical GDP of China">China's GDP</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Three_Gorges_Dam" title="Three Gorges Dam">Three Gorges Dam</a> on the Yangtze is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_the_largest_hydroelectric_power_stations" title="List of the largest hydroelectric power stations">largest hydro-electric power station in the world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> In mid-2014, the Chinese government announced it was building a multi-tier <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transport_network" title="Transport network">transport network</a>, comprising railways, roads and airports, to create a new economic belt alongside the river.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChinaEconomy_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChinaEconomy-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Yangtze flows through a wide array of ecosystems and is habitat to several <a href="/wiki/Endemism" title="Endemism">endemic</a> and threatened species including the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_alligator" title="Chinese alligator">Chinese alligator</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Yangtze_finless_porpoise" title="Yangtze finless porpoise">narrow-ridged finless porpoise</a>, and also was the home of the now extinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_river_dolphin" title="Yangtze river dolphin">Yangtze river dolphin</a> (or <i>baiji</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_paddlefish" title="Chinese paddlefish">Chinese paddlefish</a>, as well as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_sturgeon" title="Yangtze sturgeon">Yangtze sturgeon</a>, which is <a href="/wiki/Extinct_in_the_wild" title="Extinct in the wild">extinct in the wild</a>. In recent years, the river has suffered from industrial pollution, plastic pollution,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agricultural_runoff" title="Agricultural runoff">agricultural runoff</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siltation" title="Siltation">siltation</a>, and loss of wetland and lakes, which exacerbates seasonal flooding. Some sections of the river are now protected as <a href="/wiki/List_of_protected_areas_of_China" title="List of protected areas of China">nature reserves</a>. A stretch of the upstream Yangtze flowing through deep gorges in western <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a> is part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Three_Parallel_Rivers_of_Yunnan_Protected_Areas" title="Three Parallel Rivers of Yunnan Protected Areas">Three Parallel Rivers of Yunnan Protected Areas</a>, a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese">Chinese<span class="anchor" id="Chinese_name"></span><span class="anchor" id="Chinese_names"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Chinese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">Cháng Jiāng</i></span> (<span lang="zh-Hans">长江</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">長江</span>) is the official name for the Yangtze (扬子) in <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin Chinese</a>. However, because the <a href="/wiki/River_source" title="River source">source</a> of the Yangtze was not ascertained until modern times, the Chinese have given different names to the upstream sections of the river up to its confluence with the <a href="/wiki/Min_River_(Sichuan)" title="Min River (Sichuan)">Min River</a> at <a href="/wiki/Yibin" title="Yibin">Yibin</a>, Sichuan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jamieson_1911,_p._903_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jamieson_1911,_p._903-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yule_1880,_p._35_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yule_1880,_p._35-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jinsha_River" title="Jinsha River">Jinsha River</a> ("Gold Sands River") refers to the 2,308 km (1,434 mi) of the Yangtze from Yibin upstream to the confluence with the Batang River near Yushu in Qinghai, while the <a href="/wiki/Tongtian_River" title="Tongtian River">Tongtian River</a> ("River that leads to Heaven") describes the 813 km (505 mi) section from Yushu up to the confluence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuotuo_River" title="Tuotuo River">Tuotuo River</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dangqu_River" title="Dangqu River">Dangqu River</a>.
</p><p><i>Chang Jiang</i> literally means the "Long River." In <a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">Old Chinese</a>, the Yangtze was simply called <i>Jiang/Kiang</i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">江</span></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baxjiang_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-baxjiang-15">[15]</a></sup> a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_character" title="Chinese character">character</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phono-semantic_compound" title="Phono-semantic compound">phono-semantic compound</a> origin, combining the water <a href="/wiki/Radical_(Chinese_characters)" title="Radical (Chinese characters)">radical</a> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">氵</span></span> with the homophone <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">工</span></span> (now pronounced <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">gōng</i></span>, but <i>*kˤoŋ</i> in Old Chinese<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>). <i>Krong</i> was probably a word in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austroasiatic" title="Austroasiatic">Austroasiatic</a> language of local peoples such as the <a href="/wiki/Baiyue" title="Baiyue">Yue</a>. Similar to <i>*krong</i> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Vietnamese" title="Proto-Vietnamese">Proto-Vietnamese</a> and <i>krung</i> in <a href="/wiki/Mon_language" title="Mon language">Mon</a>, all meaning "river", it is related to <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_language" title="Vietnamese language">modern Vietnamese</a> <i>sông</i> (river) and <a href="/wiki/Khmer_language" title="Khmer language">Khmer</a> <i>krung</i> (city on riverside), whence <a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">Thai</a> <i>krung</i> (กรุง capital city), not <i>kôngkea</i> (water) which is from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a> root <i>gáṅgā</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a>, <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">Jiāng</i></span> had come to mean <i>any</i> river in Chinese, and this river was distinguished as the "Great River" <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">大江</span></span> (<span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">Dàjiāng</i></span>).  The epithet <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">長</span></span> (<a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified version</a> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hans">长</span></span>), means "long", was first formally applied to the river during the <a href="/wiki/Six_Dynasties" title="Six Dynasties">Six Dynasties</a> period.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Various sections of the Yangtze have local names. From Yibin to <a href="/wiki/Yichang" title="Yichang">Yichang</a>, the river through Sichuan and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chongqing_Municipality" title="Chongqing Municipality">Chongqing Municipality</a> is also known as the <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">Chuān Jiāng</i></span> (<span lang="zh">川江</span>) or "<a href="/wiki/Sichuan_River" title="Sichuan River">Sichuan River</a>."
In <a href="/wiki/Hubei" title="Hubei">Hubei</a>, the river is also called the <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">Jīng Jiāng</i></span> (<span lang="zh-Hans">荆江</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">荊江</span>) or the "Jing River" after <a href="/wiki/Jingzhou_(ancient_China)" title="Jingzhou (ancient China)">Jingzhou</a>, one of the Nine Provinces of ancient China.  In <a href="/wiki/Anhui" title="Anhui">Anhui</a>, the river takes on the local name <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">Wǎn Jiāng</i></span> after the shorthand name for Anhui, <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">wǎn</i></span> (皖).  And <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">Yángzǐ Jiāng</i></span> (<span lang="zh-Hant">揚子江</span>; <span lang="zh-Hans">扬子江</span>) or the "Yangzi River", from which the English name Yangtze is derived, is the local name for the Lower Yangtze in the region of <a href="/wiki/Yangzhou_(ancient_China)" title="Yangzhou (ancient China)">Yangzhou</a>. The name likely comes from an ancient ferry crossing called <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">Yángzǐ</i></span> or <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">Yángzǐjīn</i></span> (<span lang="zh-Hant">揚子 / 揚子津</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Europeans who arrived in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River_Delta" title="Yangtze River Delta">Yangtze River Delta</a> region applied this <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endonym" title="Endonym">local name</a> to the whole river.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jamieson_1911,_p._903_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jamieson_1911,_p._903-13">[13]</a></sup> The dividing site between upstream and <a href="/wiki/Midstream" title="Midstream">midstream</a> is considered to be at Yichang and that between midstream and downstream at <a href="/wiki/Hukou_County" title="Hukou County">Hukou</a> (<a href="/wiki/Jiujiang" title="Jiujiang">Jiujiang</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="English">English</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: English">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_map_of_China.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1371" data-file-width="1716" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Portrait_map_of_China.jpg/220px-Portrait_map_of_China.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Portrait_map_of_China.jpg/330px-Portrait_map_of_China.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Portrait_map_of_China.jpg/440px-Portrait_map_of_China.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_map_of_China.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of China depicting the Yellow River's southerly path following its stabilization by the Grand Eunuch <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Li_Xing_(eunuch)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Li Xing (eunuch) (page does not exist)">Li Xing</a>'s public works after the <a href="/wiki/1494_Yellow_River_flood" title="1494 Yellow River flood">1494 flood</a></div></div></div>
<p><span class="anchor" id="English_name"></span><span class="anchor" id="English_names"></span>
The river was called <b>Quian</b> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">江</span></span>) and <b>Quianshui</b> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">江水</span></span>) by <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> and appeared on the earliest English maps as <b>Kian</b> or <b>Kiam</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moll_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-moll-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> which derives from Cantonese, all recording <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_dialects" title="Chinese dialects">dialects</a> which preserved forms of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a> pronunciation of <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">江</span></span> as <i>Kæwng</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baxjiang_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-baxjiang-15">[15]</a></sup> By the mid-19th century, these romanizations had standardized as <b>Kiang</b>; <i>Dajiang</i>, e.g., was rendered as "Ta-Kiang." "Keeang-Koo,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bell_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-bell-23">[23]</a></sup> "Kyang Kew,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tan_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-tan-24">[24]</a></sup> "Kian-ku,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-repo_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-repo-25">[25]</a></sup> and related names derived from mistaking the Chinese term for the mouth of the Yangtze (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">江口</span></span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">p</a></small> <i>Jiāngkǒu</i>) as the name of the river itself.
</p><p>The name <b>Blue River</b> began to be applied in the 18th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moll_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-moll-21">[21]</a></sup> apparently owing to a former name of the Dam Chu<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> or Min<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> and to analogy with the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow River</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> but it was frequently explained in early English references as a 'translation' of <i>Jiang</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> <i>Jiangkou</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bell_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-bell-23">[23]</a></sup> or <i>Yangzijiang</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Very common in 18th- and 19th-century sources, the name fell out of favor due to growing awareness of its lack of <i>any</i> connection to the river's Chinese names<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jon_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-jon-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StJ_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-StJ-36">[36]</a></sup> and to the irony of its application to such a muddy waterway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StJ_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-StJ-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Matteo_Ricci" title="Matteo Ricci">Matteo Ricci</a>'s 1615 <a href="/wiki/De_Christiana_expeditione_apud_Sinas" title="De Christiana expeditione apud Sinas">Latin account</a> included descriptions of the "Ianſu" and "Ianſuchian."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chrex_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-chrex-38">[38]</a></sup> The posthumous account's translation of the name as <b>Fils de la Mer</b> ("Son of the Ocean")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chrex_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-chrex-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> shows that Ricci, who by the end of his life was fluent in literary Chinese, was introduced to it as the homophonic <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">洋子江</span></span> rather than the 'proper' <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">揚子江</span></span>. Further, although <a href="/wiki/History_of_rail_transport_in_China" title="History of rail transport in China">railroads</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shanghai_concessions" title="Shanghai concessions">Shanghai concessions</a> subsequently turned it into a backwater, <a href="/wiki/Yangzhou" title="Yangzhou"><b>Yang</b>zhou</a> was the lower river's principal port for much of the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a>, directing <a href="/wiki/Viceroy_of_Liangjiang" title="Viceroy of Liangjiang">Liangjiang</a>'s important <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_China_before_1912" title="Economic history of China before 1912">salt monopoly</a> and connecting the Yangtze with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_Canal_of_China" title="Grand Canal of China">Grand Canal</a> to Beijing. (That connection also made it one of the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River"><i>Yellow</i> River</a>'s principal ports between the floods of <a href="/wiki/1344_Yellow_River_flood" title="1344 Yellow River flood">1344</a> and the 1850s, during which time the Yellow River ran well south of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shandong_peninsula" title="Shandong peninsula">Shandong</a> and discharged into the ocean a mere few hundred kilometers from the mouth of the Yangtze.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jon_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-jon-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-repo_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-repo-25">[25]</a></sup>)
</p><p>By 1800, English cartographers such as <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Arrowsmith" title="Aaron Arrowsmith">Aaron Arrowsmith</a> had adopted the French style of the name<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> as <b>Yang-tse</b> or <b>Yang-tse Kiang</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The British diplomat <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Francis_Wade" title="Thomas Francis Wade">Thomas Wade</a> emended this to <b>Yang-tzu Chiang</b> as part of his formerly popular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wade-Giles" title="Wade-Giles">romanization of Chinese</a>, based on the <a href="/wiki/Beijing_dialect" title="Beijing dialect">Beijing dialect</a> instead of Nanjing's and first published in 1867. The spellings <b>Yangtze</b> and <b>Yangtze Kiang</b> was a compromise between the two methods adopted at the 1906 Imperial Postal Conference in Shanghai, which established <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postal_romanization" title="Postal romanization">postal romanization</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hanyu_Pinyin" title="Hanyu Pinyin">Hanyu Pinyin</a> was adopted by the PRC's <a href="/wiki/1st_National_People%27s_Congress" title="1st National People's Congress">First Congress</a> in 1958, but it was not widely employed in English outside mainland China prior to the normalization of diplomatic relations between the United States and the PRC in 1979; since that time, the spelling <b>Yangzi</b> has also been used.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tibetan">Tibetan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Tibetan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Tibetan_name"></span><span class="anchor" id="Tibetan_names"></span>
The source and upper reaches of the Yangtze are located in <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_people" title="Tibetan people">ethnic Tibetan</a> areas of <a href="/wiki/Qinghai" title="Qinghai">Qinghai</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> In Tibetan, the Tuotuo headwaters are the <i>Machu</i> (<a href="/wiki/Tibetan_script" title="Tibetan script">Tibetan</a>: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1106655535" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="uchen" lang="bo" style="font-size:1.25em; vertical-align:middle; word-wrap:break-word;">རྨ་ཆུ་</span>, <a href="/wiki/Wylie_transliteration" title="Wylie transliteration">Wylie</a>: <i>rma-chu</i>, literally "Red Water" or perhaps "Wound-[like Red] Water?")). The Tongtian is the <i>Drichu</i> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1106655535" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="uchen" style="font-size:1.25em; vertical-align:middle; word-wrap:break-word;">འབྲི་ཆུ་ </span>, ‘Bri Chu’), literally "Water of the Female <a href="/wiki/Yak" title="Yak">Yak</a>"; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transliteration_into_Chinese" title="Transliteration into Chinese">transliterated</a> into <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans">直曲</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Zhíqū</span></i>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_Yangtze_River.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4136" data-file-width="8977" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Map_of_the_Yangtze_River.gif/320px-Map_of_the_Yangtze_River.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Map_of_the_Yangtze_River.gif/480px-Map_of_the_Yangtze_River.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Map_of_the_Yangtze_River.gif/640px-Map_of_the_Yangtze_River.gif 2x" width="320"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_Yangtze_River.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Yangtze drainage basin</div></div></div>
<p>The river originates from several tributaries in the eastern part of the <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Plateau" title="Tibetan Plateau">Tibetan Plateau</a>, two of which are commonly referred to as the "source."  Traditionally, the Chinese government has recognized the source as the Tuotuo tributary at the base of a glacier lying on the west of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geladandong" title="Geladandong">Geladandong</a> Mountain in the <a href="/wiki/Tanggula_Mountains" title="Tanggula Mountains">Tanggula Mountains</a>.  This source is found at <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1073938472" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Yangtze&amp;params=33_25_44_N_91_10_57_E_region:CN-QH"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">33°25′44″N</span> <span class="longitude">91°10′57″E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">33.42889°N 91.18250°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">33.42889; 91.18250</span></span></span></a></span> and while not the furthest source of the Yangtze, it is the highest source at 5,342 m (17,526 ft) above sea level.  The true source of the Yangtze, hydrologically the longest river distance from the sea, is at Jari Hill at the head of the Dam Qu tributary, approximately 325 km (202 mi) southeast of Geladandong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winchester_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Winchester-43">[43]</a></sup> This source was only discovered in the late 20th century and lies in wetlands at <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1073938472" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Yangtze&amp;params=32_36_14_N_94_30_44_E_region:CN-QH"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">32°36′14″N</span> <span class="longitude">94°30′44″E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">32.60389°N 94.51222°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">32.60389; 94.51222</span></span></span></a></span> and 5,170 m (16,960 ft) above sea level just southeast of Chadan Township in <a href="/wiki/Zadoi_County" title="Zadoi County">Zadoi County</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yushu_Prefecture" title="Yushu Prefecture">Yushu Prefecture</a>, Qinghai.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> As the historical spiritual source of the Yangtze, the Geladandong source is still commonly referred to as the source of the Yangtze since the discovery of the Jari Hill source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Winchester_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Winchester-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>These tributaries join and the river then runs eastward through <a href="/wiki/Qinghai" title="Qinghai">Qinghai</a> (Tsinghai), turning southward down a deep valley at the border of <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a> (Szechwan) and <a href="/wiki/Tibet_Autonomous_Region" title="Tibet Autonomous Region">Tibet</a> to reach <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a>. In the course of this valley, the river's elevation drops from above 5,000 m (16,000 ft) to less than 1,000 m (3,300 ft). The headwaters of the Yangtze are situated at an elevation of about 4,900 m (16,100 ft). In its descent to sea level, the river falls to an altitude of 305 m (1,001 ft) at Yibin, Sichuan, the head of navigation for riverboats, and to 192 m (630 ft) at Chongqing (Chungking). Between Chongqing and Yichang (I-ch'ang), at an altitude of 40 m (130 ft) and a distance of about 320 km (200 mi), it passes through the spectacular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_Gorges" title="Yangtze Gorges">Yangtze Gorges</a>, which are noted for their natural beauty but are dangerous to shipping.
</p><p>It enters the basin of Sichuan at <a href="/wiki/Yibin" title="Yibin">Yibin</a>. While in the Sichuan basin, it receives several mighty tributaries, increasing its water volume significantly. It then cuts through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mount_Wushan" title="Mount Wushan">Mount Wushan</a> bordering <a href="/wiki/Chongqing" title="Chongqing">Chongqing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hubei" title="Hubei">Hubei</a> to create the famous <a href="/wiki/Three_Gorges" title="Three Gorges">Three Gorges</a>. Eastward of the Three Gorges, <a href="/wiki/Yichang" title="Yichang">Yichang</a> is the first city on the <a href="/wiki/Yangtze_Plain" title="Yangtze Plain">Yangtze Plain</a>.
</p><p>After entering Hubei province, the Yangtze receives water from a number of lakes. The largest of these lakes is <a href="/wiki/Dongting_Lake" title="Dongting Lake">Dongting Lake</a>, which is located on the border of <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a> and Hubei provinces, and is the outlet for most of the rivers in Hunan. At <a href="/wiki/Wuhan" title="Wuhan">Wuhan</a>, it receives its biggest tributary, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_River_(Hanshui)" title="Han River (Hanshui)">Han River</a>, bringing water from its northern basin as far as <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">Shaanxi</a>.
</p><p>At the northern tip of Jiangxi province, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_Poyang" title="Lake Poyang">Lake Poyang</a>, the biggest freshwater lake in China, merges into the river. The river then runs through <a href="/wiki/Anhui" title="Anhui">Anhui</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu</a>, receiving more water from innumerable smaller lakes and rivers, and finally reaches the <a href="/wiki/East_China_Sea" title="East China Sea">East China Sea</a> at Shanghai.
</p><p>Four of China's five main freshwater lakes contribute their waters to the Yangtze River. Traditionally, the upstream part of the Yangtze River refers to the section from Yibin to Yichang; the middle part refers to the section from Yichang to <a href="/wiki/Hukou_County" title="Hukou County">Hukou County</a>, where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_Poyang" title="Lake Poyang">Lake Poyang</a> meets the river; the downstream part is from Hukou to Shanghai.
</p><p>The origin of the Yangtze River has been dated by some geologists to about 45 million years ago in the <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> but this dating has been disputed.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (July 2013)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Image_gallery">Image gallery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Image gallery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geologic_history">Geologic history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Geologic history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow River</a> has fluctuated widely north and south of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shandong_peninsula" title="Shandong peninsula">Shandong peninsula</a> within the historical record, the Yangtze has remained largely static. Based on studies of <a href="/wiki/Deposition_(geology)" title="Deposition (geology)">sedimentation rates</a>, however, it is unlikely that the present discharge site predates the <a href="/wiki/Late_Miocene" title="Late Miocene">late Miocene</a> (<span title="circa">c.</span><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 11</span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megaannum" title="Megaannum">Ma</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Prior to this, its headwaters drained south into the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Tonkin" title="Gulf of Tonkin">Gulf of Tonkin</a> along or near the course of the present <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_River_(Vietnam)" title="Red River (Vietnam)">Red River</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yangzi_River_gorge.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1350" data-file-width="1800" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Yangzi_River_gorge.jpg/220px-Yangzi_River_gorge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Yangzi_River_gorge.jpg/330px-Yangzi_River_gorge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Yangzi_River_gorge.jpg/440px-Yangzi_River_gorge.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yangzi_River_gorge.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Afternoon in the jagged mountains rising from the Yangtze River gorge</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Baiyue" title="Baiyue">Baiyue</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_Wu" title="State of Wu">state of Wu</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_Yue" title="State of Yue">state of Yue</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_Chu" title="State of Chu">state of Chu</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southward_expansion_of_the_Han_Dynasty" title="Southward expansion of the Han Dynasty">Southward expansion of the Han Dynasty</a></div>
<p>The Yangtze River is important to the cultural origins of <a href="/wiki/Northern_and_southern_China" title="Northern and southern China">southern China</a> and Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Human activity has been verified in the <a href="/wiki/Three_Gorges" title="Three Gorges">Three Gorges</a> area as far back as 27,000 years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> and by the 5th millennium BC, the lower Yangtze was a major population center occupied by the <a href="/wiki/Hemudu_culture" title="Hemudu culture">Hemudu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Majiabang_culture" title="Majiabang culture">Majiabang cultures</a>, both among the earliest cultivators of rice. By the 3rd millennium BC, the successor <a href="/wiki/Liangzhu_culture" title="Liangzhu culture">Liangzhu culture</a> showed evidence of influence from the <a href="/wiki/Longshan_culture" title="Longshan culture">Longshan peoples</a> of the <a href="/wiki/North_China_Plain" title="North China Plain">North China Plain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodenough&amp;Chang_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goodenough&amp;Chang-52">[52]</a></sup> What is now thought of as <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Chinese culture</a> developed along the more fertile <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow River</a> basin; the "<a href="/wiki/Baiyue" title="Baiyue">Yue</a>" people of the lower Yangtze possessed very different traditions – <a href="/wiki/Teeth_blackening" title="Teeth blackening">blackening their teeth</a>, cutting their hair <a href="/wiki/Long_hair#Asia" title="Long hair">short</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_tattooing#China" title="History of tattooing">tattooing</a> their bodies, and living in small settlements among bamboo groves<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutcheon_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hutcheon-53">[53]</a></sup> – and were considered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hua-Yi_distinction" title="Hua-Yi distinction">barbarous</a> by the northerners.
</p><p>The Central Yangtze valley was home to sophisticated <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Later it became the earliest part of the Yangtze valley to be integrated into the North Chinese cultural sphere. (Northern Chinese were active there since the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Streitende-Reiche2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="896" data-file-width="886" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Streitende-Reiche2.jpg/220px-Streitende-Reiche2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Streitende-Reiche2.jpg/330px-Streitende-Reiche2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Streitende-Reiche2.jpg/440px-Streitende-Reiche2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Streitende-Reiche2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States</a> around 350 BC, showing the former coastline of the Yangtze delta</div></div></div>
<p>In the lower Yangtze, two <a href="/wiki/Baiyue" title="Baiyue">Yue</a> tribes, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gouwu" title="Gouwu">Gouwu</a></i> in southern <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu</a> and the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuyue" title="Yuyue">Yuyue</a></i> in northern <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a>, display increasing Zhou (i.e., North Chinese) influence starting in the 9th century BC. Traditional accounts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> credit these changes to northern refugees (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wu_Taibo" title="Wu Taibo">Taibo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zhongyong_of_Wu" title="Zhongyong of Wu">Zhongyong</a> in Wu and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Wuyi_of_Yue&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Wuyi of Yue (page does not exist)">Wuyi</a> in Yue) who assumed power over the local tribes, though these are generally assumed to be myths invented to legitimate them to other Zhou rulers. As the kingdoms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_Wu" title="State of Wu">Wu</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_Yue" title="State of Yue">Yue</a>, they were famed as fishers, shipwrights, and sword-smiths. Adopting <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a>, political institutions, and military technology, they were among the most powerful <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Chinese_states" title="Ancient Chinese states">states</a> during the later <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou</a>. In the middle Yangtze, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_Jing" title="State of Jing">state of Jing</a> seems to have begun in the upper Han River valley a minor Zhou polity, but it adapted to native culture as it expanded south and east into the Yangtze valley. In the process, it changed its name to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_Chu" title="State of Chu">Chu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Whether native or nativizing, the Yangtze states held their own against the northern Chinese homeland: some lists credit them with three of the <a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_period" title="Spring and Autumn period">Spring and Autumn period</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Five_Hegemons" title="Five Hegemons">Five Hegemons</a> and one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warring_States" title="Warring States">Warring States</a>' <a href="/wiki/Four_Lords_of_the_Warring_States" title="Four Lords of the Warring States">Four Lords</a>. They fell in against themselves, however. Chu's growing power led its rival <a href="/wiki/Jin_(Chinese_state)" title="Jin (Chinese state)">Jin</a> to support Wu as a counter. Wu successfully sacked Chu's capital <a href="/wiki/Ying_(Chu)" title="Ying (Chu)">Ying</a> in 506 BC, but Chu subsequently supported Yue in its attacks against Wu's southern flank. In 473 BC, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Goujian_of_Yue" title="King Goujian of Yue">King Goujian of Yue</a> fully annexed Wu and moved his court to its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wu_(city)" title="Wu (city)">eponymous capital</a> at modern Suzhou. In 333 BC, Chu finally united the lower Yangtze by annexing Yue, whose royal family was said to have fled south and established the <a href="/wiki/Minyue" title="Minyue">Minyue</a> kingdom in <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_Qin" title="State of Qin">Qin</a> was able to unite China by first subduing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_Ba" title="State of Ba">Ba</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_Shu" title="State of Shu">Shu</a> on the upper Yangtze in modern <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a>, giving them a strong base to attack Chu's settlements along the river.
</p><p>The state of Qin conquered the central Yangtze region, previous heartland of Chu, in 278 BC, and incorporated the region into its expanding empire. Qin then used its connections along the Yangtze River the <a href="/wiki/Xiang_River" title="Xiang River">Xiang River</a> to expand China into <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a>, Jiangxi and <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a>, setting up military commanderies along the main lines of communication. At the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collapse_of_the_Qin_Dynasty" title="Collapse of the Qin Dynasty">collapse of the Qin Dynasty</a>, these southern commanderies became the independent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanyue_Empire" title="Nanyue Empire">Nanyue Empire</a> under <a href="/wiki/Zhao_Tuo" title="Zhao Tuo">Zhao Tuo</a> while Chu and Han <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chu-Han_Contention" title="Chu-Han Contention">vied with each other</a> for control of the north.
</p><p>Since the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a>, the region of the Yangtze River grew ever more important to China's economy. The establishment of irrigation systems (the most famous one is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dujiangyan_Irrigation_System" title="Dujiangyan Irrigation System">Dujiangyan</a>, northwest of Chengdu, built during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warring_States" title="Warring States">Warring States</a> period) made agriculture very stable and productive, eventually exceeding even the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow River</a> region. The Qin and Han empires were actively engaged in the agricultural colonization of the Yangtze lowlands, maintaining a system of dikes to protect farmland from seasonal floods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> By the Song dynasty, the area along the Yangtze had become among the wealthiest and most developed parts of the country, especially in the lower reaches of the river. Early in the Qing dynasty, the region called <a href="/wiki/Jiangnan" title="Jiangnan">Jiangnan</a> (that includes the southern part of <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu</a>, the northern part of <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a>, and the southeastern part of <a href="/wiki/Anhui" title="Anhui">Anhui</a>) provided <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">3</span></span>–<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">2</span></span> of the nation's revenues.
</p><p>The Yangtze has long been the backbone of China's inland water transportation system, which remained particularly important for almost two thousand years, until the construction of the national railway network during the 20th century. The <a href="/wiki/Grand_Canal_(China)" title="Grand Canal (China)">Grand Canal</a> connects the lower Yangtze with the major cities of the <a href="/wiki/Jiangnan" title="Jiangnan">Jiangnan</a> region south of the river (<a href="/wiki/Wuxi" title="Wuxi">Wuxi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suzhou" title="Suzhou">Suzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hangzhou" title="Hangzhou">Hangzhou</a>) and with northern China (all the way from <a href="/wiki/Yangzhou" title="Yangzhou">Yangzhou</a> to Beijing). The less well known ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lingqu_Canal" title="Lingqu Canal">Lingqu Canal</a>, connecting the upper <a href="/wiki/Xiang_River" title="Xiang River">Xiang River</a> with the headwaters of the Guijiang, allowed a direct water connection from the Yangtze Basin to the <a href="/wiki/Pearl_River_Delta" title="Pearl River Delta">Pearl River Delta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historically, the Yangtze became the political boundary between north China and south China several times (see <a href="/wiki/History_of_China" title="History of China">History of China</a>) because of the difficulty of crossing the river. This occurred notably during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_and_Northern_Dynasties" title="Southern and Northern Dynasties">Southern and Northern Dynasties</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Song" title="Southern Song">Southern Song</a>. Many battles took place along the river, the most famous being the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Red_Cliffs" title="Battle of Red Cliffs">Battle of Red Cliffs</a> in 208 AD during the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms" title="Three Kingdoms">Three Kingdoms</a> period.
</p><p>The Yangtze was the site of naval battles between the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jurchen_people" title="Jurchen people">Jurchen</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115-1234)" title="Jin dynasty (1115-1234)">Jin</a> during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jin%E2%80%93Song_wars" title="Jin–Song wars">Jin–Song wars</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Caishi" title="Battle of Caishi">Battle of Caishi</a> of 1161, the ships of the Jin emperor <a href="/wiki/Wanyan_Liang" title="Wanyan Liang">Wanyan Liang</a> clashed with the <a href="/wiki/Naval_history_of_China" title="Naval history of China">Song fleet</a> on the Yangtze. Song soldiers fired bombs of <a href="/wiki/Lime_(material)" title="Lime (material)">lime</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> using trebuchets at the Jurchen warships. The battle was a Song victory that halted the invasion by the Jin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tangdao" title="Battle of Tangdao">Battle of Tangdao</a> was another Yangtze naval battle in the same year.
</p><p>Politically, <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> was the capital of China several times, although most of the time its territory only covered the southeastern part of China, such as the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Wu" title="Eastern Wu">Wu kingdom</a> in the Three Kingdoms period, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Jin_Dynasty" title="Eastern Jin Dynasty">Eastern Jin Dynasty</a>, and during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_and_Northern_Dynasties" title="Southern and Northern Dynasties">Southern and Northern Dynasties</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_Dynasties_and_Ten_Kingdoms" title="Five Dynasties and Ten Kingdoms">Five Dynasties and Ten Kingdoms</a> periods. Only the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming</a> occupied most parts of China from their capital at <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a>, though it later moved the capital to Beijing. The <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_(1912%E2%80%931949)" title="Republic of China (1912–1949)">ROC</a> capital was located in <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> in the periods 1911–12, 1927–37, and 1945–49.
</p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto;overflow:hidden;width:auto;max-width:5008px"><div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize" style="overflow:auto"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anonymous-Ten_Thousand_Miles_of_the_Yangtze_River.jpg" title="Ten Thousand Miles of the Yangtze River, a Ming dynasty landscape painting"><img alt="" data-file-height="470" data-file-width="22517" decoding="async" height="104" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Anonymous-Ten_Thousand_Miles_of_the_Yangtze_River.jpg/5000px-Anonymous-Ten_Thousand_Miles_of_the_Yangtze_River.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Anonymous-Ten_Thousand_Miles_of_the_Yangtze_River.jpg/7500px-Anonymous-Ten_Thousand_Miles_of_the_Yangtze_River.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Anonymous-Ten_Thousand_Miles_of_the_Yangtze_River.jpg/10000px-Anonymous-Ten_Thousand_Miles_of_the_Yangtze_River.jpg 2x" width="5000"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anonymous-Ten_Thousand_Miles_of_the_Yangtze_River.jpg" title="File:Anonymous-Ten Thousand Miles of the Yangtze River.jpg"> </a></div><i>Ten Thousand Miles of the Yangtze River</i>, a <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> landscape painting</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Age_of_steam">Age of steam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Age of steam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Steamboats_on_the_Yangtze_River" title="Steamboats on the Yangtze River">Steamboats on the Yangtze River</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/China_Station" title="China Station">China Station</a></div>
<p>The first merchant steamer in <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">China</a>, the <i>Jardine</i>, was built to order for the firm of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jardine_Matheson_Holdings" title="Jardine Matheson Holdings">Jardine, Matheson &amp; Co.</a> in 1835. She was a small vessel intended for use as a mail and passenger carrier between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lintin_Island" title="Lintin Island">Lintin Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Macau" title="Portuguese Macau">Macao</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Huangpu_District,_Guangzhou" title="Huangpu District, Guangzhou">Whampoa</a>. However, the Chinese, draconian in their application of the rules relating to foreign vessels, were unhappy about a "fire-ship" steaming up the Canton River. The acting Governor-General of Kwangtung issued an edict warning that she would be fired on if she attempted the trip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-62">[62]</a></sup> On the <i>Jardine's</i> first trial run from Lintin Island the forts on both sides of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bocca_Tigris" title="Bocca Tigris">Bogue</a> opened fire and she was forced to turn back. The Chinese authorities issued a further warning insisting that the ship leave Chinese waters. The <i>Jardine</i> in any case needed repairs and was sent to <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><br/> Subsequently, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_Temple,_3rd_Viscount_Palmerston" title="Henry Temple, 3rd Viscount Palmerston">Lord Palmerston</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Affairs" title="Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs">Foreign Secretary</a> decided mainly on the "suggestions" of <a href="/wiki/William_Jardine_(merchant)" title="William Jardine (merchant)">William Jardine</a> to declare war on China. In mid-1840, a large fleet of warships appeared on the China coast, and with the first cannonball fired at a British ship, the <i>Royal Saxon</i>, the British started the <a href="/wiki/First_Opium_War" title="First Opium War">first of the Opium Wars</a>. <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> warships destroyed numerous shore batteries and Chinese warships, laying waste to several coastal forts along the way. Eventually, they pushed their way up north close enough to threaten the <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_City" title="Forbidden City">Imperial Palace</a> in <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Peking</a> itself. The Chinese imperial government quickly gave in to the demands of the British. British military superiority was clearly evident during the conflict. British warships, constructed using such innovations as steam power combined with sail and the use of iron in shipbuilding, wreaked havoc on Chinese junks; such ships (like the <i>Nemesis</i>) were not only virtually indestructible but also highly mobile and able to support a gun platform with very heavy guns. In addition, the British troops were armed with modern muskets and cannons, unlike the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qing_Dynasty" title="Qing Dynasty">Qing</a> forces. After the British captured <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Canton</a>, they sailed up the Yangtze and seized the tax barges, a significant blow to the Imperial government as it slashed the revenue of the imperial court in Beijing to just a small fraction of what it had been.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1842 the Qing authorities sued for peace, which concluded with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Nanking" title="Treaty of Nanking">Treaty of Nanking</a> signed on a warship in the river, negotiated in August of that year and ratified in 1843. In the treaty, China was forced to pay an indemnity to Britain, open five treaty ports to all foreign nations, and cede <a href="/wiki/British_Hong_Kong" title="British Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victoria_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Victoria of the United Kingdom">Queen Victoria</a>. In the supplementary <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_the_Bogue" title="Treaty of the Bogue">Treaty of the Bogue</a>, the Qing Empire also recognized Britain as an equal to China and gave British subjects extraterritorial privileges in <a href="/wiki/Treaty_ports" title="Treaty ports">treaty ports</a>. The China Navigation Company was an early shipping company founded in 1876 in London, initially to trade up the Yangtze River from their Shanghai base with passengers and cargo. Chinese coastal trade started shortly after, and in 1883 a regular service to Australia was initiated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
