<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._and_French_conflicts">U.S. and French conflicts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: U.S. and French conflicts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The US, at the same time, wanting to protect its interests and expand trade, ventured the <a href="/wiki/USS_Wachusett_(1861)" title="USS Wachusett (1861)">USS <i>Wachusett</i></a> six hundred miles up the river to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hankou_District" title="Hankou District">Hankow</a> sometime in the 1860s, while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USS_Ashuelot_(1865)" title="USS Ashuelot (1865)">USS <i>Ashuelot</i></a>, a sidewheeler, made her way up the river to <a href="/wiki/Yichang" title="Yichang">Yichang</a> in 1874. The first <a href="/wiki/USS_Monocacy_(1864)" title="USS Monocacy (1864)">USS <i>Monocacy</i></a>, a sidewheel gunboat, began charting the Yangtze River in 1871. The first <a href="/wiki/USS_Palos_(1865)" title="USS Palos (1865)">USS <i>Palos</i></a>, an armed tug, was on <a href="/wiki/Asiatic_Squadron" title="Asiatic Squadron">Asiatic Station</a> into 1891, cruising the Chinese and Japanese coasts, visiting the open treaty ports and making occasional voyages up the Yangtze River. From June to September 1891, anti-foreign riots up the Yangtze forced the warship to make an extended voyage as far as <a href="/wiki/Hankou" title="Hankou">Hankou</a>, 600 miles upriver. Stopping at each open treaty port, the gunboat cooperated with naval vessels of other nations and repairing damage. She then operated along the north and central China coast and on the lower Yangtze until June 1892. The cessation of bloodshed with the <a href="/wiki/Taiping_Rebellion" title="Taiping Rebellion">Taiping Rebellion</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Europeans" title="Europeans">Europeans</a> put more steamers on the river. The French engaged the Chinese in war over the rule of <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Sino-French_War" title="Sino-French War">Sino-French Wars</a> of the 1880s emerged with the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Shipu" title="Battle of Shipu">Battle of Shipu</a> having French cruisers in the lower Yangtze.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USS_Luzon_(PR-7)_docked_in_China,_in_the_1930s.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="333" data-file-width="565" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/USS_Luzon_%28PR-7%29_docked_in_China%2C_in_the_1930s.jpg/220px-USS_Luzon_%28PR-7%29_docked_in_China%2C_in_the_1930s.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/USS_Luzon_%28PR-7%29_docked_in_China%2C_in_the_1930s.jpg/330px-USS_Luzon_%28PR-7%29_docked_in_China%2C_in_the_1930s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/USS_Luzon_%28PR-7%29_docked_in_China%2C_in_the_1930s.jpg/440px-USS_Luzon_%28PR-7%29_docked_in_China%2C_in_the_1930s.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USS_Luzon_(PR-7)_docked_in_China,_in_the_1930s.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/USS_Luzon_(PG-47)" title="USS Luzon (PG-47)">USS <i>Luzon</i></a></div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_China_Navigation_Company" title="The China Navigation Company">The China Navigation Company</a> was an early shipping company founded in 1876 in London, initially to trade up the Yangtze River from their Shanghai base with passengers and cargo. Chinese coastal trade started shortly after and in 1883 a regular service to Australia was initiated. Most of the company's ships were seized by Japan in 1941 and services did not resume until 1946. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Dollar" title="Robert Dollar">Robert Dollar</a> was a later shipping magnate, who became enormously influential moving Californian and Canadian lumber to the Chinese and Japanese market.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Yichang" title="Yichang">Yichang</a>, or Ichang, 1,600 km (990 mi) from the sea, is the head of navigation for river steamers; oceangoing vessels may navigate the river to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hankow" title="Hankow">Hankow</a>, a distance of almost 1,000 km (620 mi) from the sea. For about 320 km (200 mi) inland from its mouth, the river is virtually at sea level.
</p><p>The Chinese Government, too, had steamers. It had its own naval fleet, the <a href="/wiki/Nanyang_Fleet" title="Nanyang Fleet">Nanyang Fleet</a>, which fell prey to the French fleet. The Chinese would rebuild its fleet, only to be ravaged by another war with <a href="/wiki/First_Sino-Japanese_War" title="First Sino-Japanese War">Japan (1895)</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xinhai_Revolution" title="Xinhai Revolution">Revolution (1911)</a> and ongoing inefficiency and corruption. Chinese companies ran their own steamers, but were second tier to European operations at the time.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Navigation_on_the_upper_river">Navigation on the upper river</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Navigation on the upper river">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yangtze_kiang_1915.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="854" data-file-width="4534" decoding="async" height="41" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Yangtze_kiang_1915.jpg/220px-Yangtze_kiang_1915.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Yangtze_kiang_1915.jpg/330px-Yangtze_kiang_1915.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Yangtze_kiang_1915.jpg/440px-Yangtze_kiang_1915.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yangtze_kiang_1915.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Yangtze in 1915</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cruise_boats_on_yangtze.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Cruise_boats_on_yangtze.jpg/220px-Cruise_boats_on_yangtze.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Cruise_boats_on_yangtze.jpg/330px-Cruise_boats_on_yangtze.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Cruise_boats_on_yangtze.jpg/440px-Cruise_boats_on_yangtze.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cruise_boats_on_yangtze.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cruise boats on Yangtze</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_vehicle_carrier_on_yangtze.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/A_vehicle_carrier_on_yangtze.jpg/220px-A_vehicle_carrier_on_yangtze.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/A_vehicle_carrier_on_yangtze.jpg/330px-A_vehicle_carrier_on_yangtze.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/A_vehicle_carrier_on_yangtze.jpg/440px-A_vehicle_carrier_on_yangtze.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_vehicle_carrier_on_yangtze.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A vehicle carrier on Yangtze</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_container_carrier_on_yangtze.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/A_container_carrier_on_yangtze.jpg/220px-A_container_carrier_on_yangtze.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/A_container_carrier_on_yangtze.jpg/330px-A_container_carrier_on_yangtze.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/A_container_carrier_on_yangtze.jpg/440px-A_container_carrier_on_yangtze.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_container_carrier_on_yangtze.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A container carrier on Yangtze</div></div></div>
<p>Steamers came late to the upper river, the section stretching from Yichang to Chongqing. Freshets from Himalayan snowmelt created treacherous seasonal currents. But summer was better navigationally and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Three_gorges" title="Three gorges">three gorges</a>, described as an "150-mile passage which is like the narrow throat of an hourglass," posed hazardous threats of crosscurrents, whirlpools and eddies, creating significant challenges to steamship efforts. Furthermore, Chongqing is 700 – 800 feet above sea level, requiring powerful engines to make the upriver climb. Junk travel accomplished the upriver feat by employing 70–80 trackers, men hitched to hawsers who physically pulled ships upriver through some of the most risky and deadly sections of the three gorges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Achibald John Little took an interest in Upper Yangtze navigation when in 1876, the <a href="/wiki/Chefoo_Convention" title="Chefoo Convention">Chefoo Convention</a> opened Chongqing to consular residence but stipulated that foreign trade might only commence once steamships had succeeded in ascending the river to that point. Little formed the Upper Yangtze Steam Navigation Co., Ltd. and built <i>Kuling</i> but his attempts to take the vessel further upriver than Yichang were thwarted by the Chinese authorities who were concerned about the potential loss of transit duties, competition to their native junk trade and physical damage to their crafts caused by steamship wakes.  <i>Kuling</i> was sold to China Merchants Steam Navigation Company for lower river service.  In 1890, the Chinese government agreed to open Chongqing to foreign trade as long as it was restricted to native crafts.  In 1895, the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Shimonoseki" title="Treaty of Shimonoseki">Treaty of Shimonoseki</a> provided a provision which opened Chongqing fully to foreign trade.  Little took up residence in Chongqing and built <i>Leechuan</i>, to tackle the gorges in 1898.  In March <i>Leechuan</i> completed the upriver journey to Chongqing but not without the assistance of trackers.  <i>Leechuan</i> was not designed for cargo or passengers and if Little wanted to take his vision one step further, he required an expert pilot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> In 1898, Little persuaded Captain <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Cornell_Plant" title="Samuel Cornell Plant">Samuel Cornell Plant</a> to come out to China to lend his expertise.  Captain Plant had just completed navigation of <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Persia</a>'s Upper <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karun_River" title="Karun River">Karun River</a> and took up Little's offer to assess the Upper Yangtze on <i>Leechuan</i> at the end of 1898. With Plant's design input, Little had SS <i>Pioneer</i> built with Plant in command.  In June 1900, Plant was the first to successfully pilot a merchant steamer on the Upper Yangtze from Yichang to Chongqing.  <i>Pioneer</i> was sold to <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> after its first run due to threat from the <a href="/wiki/Boxer_Rebellion" title="Boxer Rebellion">Boxer Rebellion</a> and renamed HMS <i>Kinsha</i>.  Germany's steamship effort that same year on SS <i>Suixing</i> ended in catastrophe.  On <i>Suixing's</i> maiden voyage, the vessel hit a rock and sunk, killing its captain and ending realistic hopes of regular commercial steam service on the Upper Yangtze.  In 1908, local Sichuan merchants and their government partnered with Captain Plant to form Sichuan Steam Navigation Company becoming the first successful service between Yichang and Chongqing.  Captain Plant designed and commanded its two ships, SS <i>Shutung</i> and SS <i>Shuhun</i>. Other Chinese vessels came onto the run and by 1915, foreign ships expressed their interest too.  Plant was appointed by <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Maritime_Customs_Service" title="Chinese Maritime Customs Service">Chinese Maritime Customs Service</a> as First Senior River Inspector in 1915.  In this role, Plant installed navigational marks and established signaling systems.  He also wrote <i>Handbook for the Guidance of Shipmasters on the Ichang-Chungking Section of the Yangtze River</i>, a detailed and illustrated account of the Upper Yangtze's currents, rocks, and other hazards with navigational instruction.  Plant trained hundreds of Chinese and foreign pilots and issued licenses and worked with the Chinese government to make the river safer in 1917 by removing some of the most difficult obstacles and threats with explosives. In August 1917, British Asiatic Petroleum became the first foreign merchant steamship on the Upper Yangtze.  Commercial firms, Robert Dollar Company, <a href="/wiki/Jardine_Matheson" title="Jardine Matheson">Jardine Matheson</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Butterfield_and_Swire" title="Butterfield and Swire">Butterfield and Swire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Standard_Oil" title="Standard Oil">Standard Oil</a> added their own steamers on the river between 1917 and 1919.  Between 1918 and 1919, Sichuan warlord violence and escalating civil war put Sichuan Steam Navigational Company out of business.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> <i>Shutung</i> was commandeered by warlords and <i>Shuhun</i> was brought down river to Shanghai for safekeeping.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> In 1921, when Captain Plant died at sea while returning home to England, a Plant Memorial Fund was established to perpetuate Plant's name and contributions to Upper Yangtze navigation.  The largest shipping companies in service, Butterfield &amp; Swire, Jardine Matheson, Standard Oil, Mackenzie &amp; Co., Asiatic Petroleum, Robert Dollar, China Merchants S.N. Co. and British-American Tobacco Co., contributed alongside international friends and Chinese pilots.  In 1924, a 50-foot granite pyramidal obelisk was erected in Xintan, on the site of Captain Plant's home, in a Chinese community of pilots and junk owners.  One face of the monument is inscribed in Chinese and another in English.  Though recently relocated to higher ground ahead of the Three Gorges Dam, the monument still stands overlooking the Upper Yangtze River near Yichang, a rare collective tribute to a westerner in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Until 1881, the <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and China coastal and river services were operated by several companies. In that year, however, these were merged into the Indo-China Steam Navigation Company Ltd, a public company under the management of Jardine's. The <a href="/wiki/Jardine_Matheson" title="Jardine Matheson">Jardine</a> company pushed inland up the Yangtsze River on which a specially designed fleet was built to meet all requirements of the river trade. Jardine's established an enviable reputation for the efficient handling of shipping. As a result, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mail_Steam_Packet_Company" title="Royal Mail Steam Packet Company">Royal Mail Steam Packet Company</a> invited the firm to attend to the Agency of their Shire Line, which operated in the Far East. Standard Oil ran the tankers Mei Ping, Mei An and Mei Hsia, which were collectively destroyed on December 12, 1937, when Japanese warplanes bombed and sank the U.S.S. Panay. One of the Standard Oil captains who survived this attack had served on the Upper River for 14 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Navy_ships">Navy ships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Navy ships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Japanese_cruiser_Izumo_in_Shanghai.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2026" data-file-width="1981" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Japanese_cruiser_Izumo_in_Shanghai.jpg/170px-Japanese_cruiser_Izumo_in_Shanghai.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Japanese_cruiser_Izumo_in_Shanghai.jpg/255px-Japanese_cruiser_Izumo_in_Shanghai.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Japanese_cruiser_Izumo_in_Shanghai.jpg/340px-Japanese_cruiser_Izumo_in_Shanghai.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Japanese_cruiser_Izumo_in_Shanghai.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Navy" title="Imperial Japanese Navy">Imperial Japanese Navy</a> <a href="/wiki/Armored_cruiser" title="Armored cruiser">armored cruiser</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Japanese_cruiser_Izumo" title="Japanese cruiser Izumo">Izumo</a></i> in Shanghai in 1937. She sank <a href="/wiki/Riverboat" title="Riverboat">riverboats</a> on the Yangtze in 1941.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/USS_Asheville_(PG-21)" title="USS Asheville (PG-21)">USS Asheville (PG-21)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yangtze_Patrol" title="Yangtze Patrol">Yangtze Patrol</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_Incident" title="Yangtze Incident">Yangtze Incident</a></div>
<p>With the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_Ports" title="Treaty Ports">Treaty Ports</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Great_power" title="Great power">European powers</a> and Japan were allowed to sail navy ships into China's waters. The British, Americans, and French did this. A full international fleet featured on Chinese waters: <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austro-Hungarian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Portugal" title="Kingdom of Portugal">Portuguese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy" title="Kingdom of Italy">Italian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian</a> and <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German</a> navy ships came to Shanghai and the treaty ports. The Japanese engaged in open warfare with the Chinese over conquest of the Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qing_Empire" title="Qing Empire">Qing Empire</a> in the <a href="/wiki/First_Sino-Japanese_War" title="First Sino-Japanese War">First Sino-Japanese War</a> in 1894–1895, and with Russia over Qing Empire territory in the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Japanese_War" title="Russo-Japanese War">Russo-Japanese War</a> of 1904–1905. Incidentally, both the <a href="/wiki/French_Navy" title="French Navy">French</a> and <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Navy" title="Imperial Japanese Navy">Japanese</a> navies were heavily involved in running <a href="/wiki/Opium" title="Opium">opium</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Narcotics" title="Narcotics">narcotics</a> to Shanghai, where it was refined into <a href="/wiki/Morphine" title="Morphine">morphine</a>. It was then transhipped by liner back to <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Marseille</a> and France (i.e. <a href="/wiki/French_Connection" title="French Connection">French Connection</a>) for processing in <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">Germany</a> and eventual sale in the U.S. or Europe.
</p><p>In 1909 the gunboat <a href="/wiki/USS_Samar_(PG-41)" title="USS Samar (PG-41)">USS <i>Samar</i></a> changed station to Shanghai, where she regularly patrolled the lower Yangtze River up to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanking" title="Nanking">Nanking</a> and Wuhu. Following an anti-foreign riots in <a href="/wiki/Changsha" title="Changsha">Changsha</a> in April 1910, which destroyed a number of missions and merchant warehouses, Samar sailed up the Yangtze River to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hankow" title="Hankow">Hankow</a> and then Changsa to show the flag and help restore order. The gunboat was also administratively assigned to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asiatic_Fleet" title="Asiatic Fleet">Asiatic Fleet</a> that year, which had been reestablished by the Navy to better protect, in the words of the Bureau of Navigation, "American interests in the Orient." After returning to Shanghai in August, she sailed up river again the following summer, passing Wuhu in June but then running aground off Kichau on July 1, 1911.
</p><p>After staying stuck in the mud for two weeks, Samar broke free and sailed back down river to coal ship. Returning upriver, the gunboat reached Hankow in August and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ichang" title="Ichang">Ichang</a> in September where she wintered over owing to both the dry season and the outbreak of rebellion at <a href="/wiki/Wuchang_District" title="Wuchang District">Wuchang</a> in October 1911. Tensions eased and the gunboat turned downriver in July 1912, arriving at Shanghai in October. Samar patrolled the lower Yangtze after fighting broke out in the summer 1913, a precursor to a decade of conflict between provincial <a href="/wiki/Warlord" title="Warlord">warlords</a> in China. In 1919, she was placed on the disposal list at Shanghai following a collision with a Yangtze River steamer that damaged her bow.
</p><p>The Spanish boats were replaced in the 1920s by <a href="/wiki/USS_Luzon_(PG-47)" title="USS Luzon (PG-47)">USS <i>Luzon</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/USS_Mindanao_(PR-8)" title="USS Mindanao (PR-8)">USS <i>Mindanao</i></a> were the largest, <a href="/wiki/USS_Oahu_(PR-6)" title="USS Oahu (PR-6)">USS <i>Oahu</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/USS_Panay_(PR-5)" title="USS Panay (PR-5)">USS <i>Panay</i></a> next in size, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USS_Guam_(PG-43)" title="USS Guam (PG-43)">USS <i>Guam</i></a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USS_Tutuila_(PG-44)" title="USS Tutuila (PG-44)">USS <i>Tutuila</i></a> the smallest. China in the first fifty years of the 20th century, was in low-grade chaos. Warlords, revolutions, natural disasters, civil war and invasions contributed. Yangtze boats were involved in the <a href="/wiki/Nanking_incident_of_1927" title="Nanking incident of 1927">Nanking incident of 1927</a> when the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Communist_Party" title="Chinese Communist Party">Communists</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Revolutionary_Army" title="National Revolutionary Army">Nationalists</a> broke into open war. The <a href="/wiki/Chiang_Kai-shek" title="Chiang Kai-shek">Chiang Kai-shek</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shanghai_massacre_of_1927" title="Shanghai massacre of 1927">massacre of the Communists</a> in Shanghai in 1927 furthered the unrest, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps" title="United States Marine Corps">U.S. Marines</a> with tanks were landed. River steamers were popular targets for both Nationalists and Communists, and peasants who would take periodic pot-shots at vessels. During the course of service the second <a href="/wiki/USS_Palos_(PG-16)" title="USS Palos (PG-16)">USS <i>Palos</i></a> protected American interests in China down the entire length of the Yangtze, at times convoying U.S. and foreign vessels on the river, evacuating American citizens during periods of disturbance and in general giving credible presence to U.S. consulates and residences in various Chinese cities. In the period of great unrest in central China in the 1920s, Palos was especially busy patrolling the upper Yangtze against bands of warlord soldiers and outlaws. The warship engaged in continuous patrol operations between Ichang and <a href="/wiki/Chongqing" title="Chongqing">Chungking</a> throughout 1923, supplying armed guards to merchant ships, and protecting Americans at Chungking while that city was under siege by a warlord army.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> had a series of <a href="/wiki/Insect-class_gunboat" title="Insect-class gunboat">Insect-class gunboats</a> which patrolled between Chungking and Shanghai. Cruisers and destroyers and <a href="/wiki/Fly-class_gunboat" title="Fly-class gunboat">Fly-class gunboats</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> also patrolled. The most infamous incident was when <i>Panay</i> and <a href="/wiki/HMS_Bee_(1915)" title="HMS Bee (1915)">HMS <i>Bee</i></a> in 1937, were <a href="/wiki/Dive_bomber" title="Dive bomber">divebombed</a> by <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Army_Air_Service" title="Imperial Japanese Army Air Service">Japanese airplanes</a> during the notorious <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanking_massacre" title="Nanking massacre">Nanking massacre</a>. Westerners were forced to leave areas neighboring the Yangtze River with the Japanese takeover in 1941. The former steamers were either sabotaged or pressed into Japanese or Chinese service. Probably the most curious incident involved <a href="/wiki/HMS_Amethyst_(F116)" title="HMS Amethyst (F116)">HMS <i>Amethyst</i></a> in 1949 during the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War" title="Chinese Civil War">Chinese Civil War</a> between <a href="/wiki/Kuomintang" title="Kuomintang">Kuomintang</a> and <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Liberation_Army" title="People's Liberation Army">People's Liberation Army</a> forces; and led to the award of the <a href="/wiki/Dickin_Medal" title="Dickin Medal">Dickin Medal</a> to the ship's cat <a href="/wiki/Simon_(cat)" title="Simon (cat)">Simon</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_events">Contemporary events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Contemporary events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In August 2019, Welsh adventurer <a href="/wiki/Ash_Dykes" title="Ash Dykes">Ash Dykes</a> became the first person to complete the 4,000-mile (6,437 km) trek along the course of the river, walking for 352 days from its source to its mouth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrology">Hydrology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Hydrology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Periodic_floods">Periodic floods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Periodic floods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_deadliest_floods" title="List of deadliest floods">List of deadliest floods</a></div>
<p>Tens of millions of people live in the floodplain of the Yangtze valley, an area that naturally floods every summer and is habitable only because it is protected by river dikes. The floods large enough to overflow the dikes have caused great distress to those who live and farm there. Floods of note include those of 1931, 1954, and 1998.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/1931_China_floods" title="1931 China floods">1931 Central China floods</a> or the Central China floods of 1931 were a series of floods that occurred in the Republic of China. The floods are generally considered among the deadliest natural disasters ever recorded, and almost certainly the deadliest of the 20th century (when pandemics and famines are discounted). Estimates of the total death toll range from 145,000 to between 3.7 million and 4 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The Yangtze again flooded in <a href="/wiki/1935_Yangtze_flood" title="1935 Yangtze flood">1935</a>, causing great loss of life.
</p><p>From June to September 1954, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1954_Yangtze_River_floods" title="1954 Yangtze River floods">Yangtze River Floods</a> were a series of catastrophic floodings that occurred mostly in Hubei Province. Due to unusually high volume of precipitation as well as an extraordinarily long rainy season in the middle stretch of the Yangtze River late in the spring of 1954, the river started to rise above its usual level in around late June. Despite efforts to open three important flood gates to alleviate the rising water by diverting it, the flood level continued to rise until it hit the historic high of 44.67 m in Jingzhou, Hubei and 29.73 m in Wuhan. The number of dead from this flood was estimated at 33,000, including those who died of plague in the aftermath of the disaster.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1998_Yangtze_River_floods" title="1998 Yangtze River floods">1998 Yangtze River floods</a> were a series of major floods that lasted from middle of June to the beginning of September 1998 along the Yangtze.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnews_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnews-74">[74]</a></sup>
In the summer of 1998, China experienced massive flooding of parts of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River" title="Yangtze River">Yangtze River</a>, resulting in 3,704 dead, 15 million homeless and $26 billion in economic loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Other sources report a total loss of 4150 people, and 180 million people were affected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spig_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-spig-76">[76]</a></sup> A staggering 25 million acres (100,000 km<sup>2</sup>) were evacuated, 13.3 million houses were damaged or destroyed.  The floods caused $26 billion in damages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spig_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-spig-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/2016_China_floods" title="2016 China floods">2016 China floods</a> caused US$22 billion in damages.
</p><p>In 2020, the Yangtze river saw the heaviest rainfall since 1961, with a 79% increase in June and July compared to the average for the period over the previous 41 years. A new theory suggested that abrupt reduction in emissions of greenhouse gases and aerosols, caused by shutdowns during the COVID-19 pandemic, was a key cause of the intense downpours. Over the past decades rainfall had decreased due to increase of aerosols in the atmosphere, and lower greenhouse gas emissions in 2020 caused the opposite effect – a major increase in rain. Such a dramatic reduction of aerosols caused a dramatic change in the various components of the climate system, but such sudden change of the climate system would be very different from changes in response to continuous but gradual policy-driven emissions reductions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Degradation_of_the_river">Degradation of the river</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Degradation of the river">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_resources_in_China#Water_quality" title="Water resources in China">Water resources in China § Water quality</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yangtze-Ships.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Yangtze-Ships.JPG/220px-Yangtze-Ships.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Yangtze-Ships.JPG/330px-Yangtze-Ships.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Yangtze-Ships.JPG/440px-Yangtze-Ships.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yangtze-Ships.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Barges on the river</div></div></div>
<p>Beginning in the 1950s, dams and dikes were built for flood control, land reclamation, irrigation, and control of diseases vectors such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_fluke" title="Blood fluke">blood flukes</a> that caused <a href="/wiki/Schistosomiasis" title="Schistosomiasis">Schistosomiasis</a>. More than a hundred lakes were thusly cut off from the main river.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WWF_UK_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-WWF_UK-78">[78]</a></sup> There were gates between the lakes that could be opened during floods. However, farmers and settlements encroached on the land next to the lakes although it was forbidden to settle there. When floods came, it proved impossible to open the gates since it would have caused substantial destruction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Thus the lakes partially or completely dried up. For example, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Baidang_Lake&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Baidang Lake (page does not exist)">Baidang Lake</a> shrunk from 100 square kilometers (39 sq mi) in the 1950s to 40 square kilometers (15 sq mi) in 2005. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Zhangdu_Lake&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Zhangdu Lake (page does not exist)">Zhangdu Lake</a> dwindled to one quarter of its original size. Natural fisheries output in the two lakes declined sharply. Only a few large lakes, such as <a href="/wiki/Poyang_Lake" title="Poyang Lake">Poyang Lake</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dongting_Lake" title="Dongting Lake">Dongting Lake</a>, remained connected to the Yangtze. Cutting off the other lakes that had served as natural buffers for floods increased the damage done by floods further downstream. Furthermore, the natural flow of migratory fish was obstructed and biodiversity across the whole basin decreased dramatically. Intensive farming of fish in ponds spread using one type of <a href="/wiki/Carp" title="Carp">carp</a> who thrived in <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophic</a> water conditions and who feeds on algae, causing widespread pollution. The pollution was exacerbated by the discharge of waste from pig farms as well as of untreated industrial and municipal sewage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WWF_UK_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-WWF_UK-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakes_reunited_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lakes_reunited-80">[80]</a></sup> In September 2012, the Yangtze river near Chongqing turned red from pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yangtze_River_Turns_Red_and_Turns_Up_a_Mystery_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yangtze_River_Turns_Red_and_Turns_Up_a_Mystery-81">[81]</a></sup> The erection of the Three Gorges Dam has created an impassable "iron barrier" that has led to a great reduction in the biodiversity of the river. Yangtze sturgeon use seasonal changes in the flow of the river to signal when is it time to migrate. However, these seasonal changes will be greatly reduced by dams and diversions. Other animals facing immediate threat of extinction are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baiji_dolphin" title="Baiji dolphin">baiji dolphin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yangtze_finless_porpoise" title="Yangtze finless porpoise">narrow-ridged finless porpoise</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_alligator" title="Yangtze alligator">Yangtze alligator</a>. These animals numbers went into freefall from the combined effects of accidental catches during fishing, river traffic, habitat loss and pollution. In 2006 the baiji dolphin became extinct; the world lost an entire genus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2020, a sweeping law was passed by the Chinese government to protect the ecology of the river. The new laws include strengthening ecological protection rules for hydropower projects along the river, banning chemical plants within 1 kilometer of the river, relocating polluting industries, severely restricting sand mining as well as a complete fishing ban on all the natural waterways of the river, including all its major tributaries and lakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contribution_to_ocean_pollution">Contribution to ocean pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Contribution to ocean pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Yangtze River produces more <a href="/wiki/Marine_plastic_pollution" title="Marine plastic pollution">ocean plastic pollution</a> than any other, according to <a href="/wiki/The_Ocean_Cleanup" title="The Ocean Cleanup">The Ocean Cleanup</a>, a Dutch environmental research foundation that focuses on <a href="/wiki/Marine_pollution" title="Marine pollution">ocean pollution</a>. Together with 9 other rivers, the Yangtze transports 90% of all the plastic that reaches the oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reconnecting_lakes">Reconnecting lakes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Reconnecting lakes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2002 a pilot program was initiated to reconnect lakes to the Yangtze with the objective to increase biodiversity and to alleviate flooding. The first lakes to be reconnected in 2004 were <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Zhangdu_Lake&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Zhangdu Lake (page does not exist)">Zhangdu Lake</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honghu_Lake" title="Honghu Lake">Honghu Lake</a>, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tian%27e-Zhou&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tian'e-Zhou (page does not exist)">Tian'e-Zhou</a> in Hubei on the middle Yangtze. In 2005 Baidang Lake in <a href="/wiki/Anhui" title="Anhui">Anhui</a> was also reconnected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakes_reunited_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lakes_reunited-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Reconnecting the lakes improved water quality and fish were able to migrate from the river into the lake, replenishing their numbers and genetic stock. The trial also showed that reconnecting the lake reduced flooding. The new approach also benefitted the farmers economically. Pond farmers switched to natural fish feed, which helped them breed better-quality fish that can be sold for more, increasing their income by 30%. Based on the successful pilot project, other provincial governments emulated the experience and also reestablished connections to lakes that had previously been cut off from the river. In 2005 a Yangtze Forum has been established bringing together 13 riparian provincial governments to manage the river from source to sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> In 2006 China's Ministry of Agriculture made it a national policy to reconnect the Yangtze River with its lakes. As of 2010, provincial governments in five provinces and Shanghai set up a network of 40 effective protected areas, covering 16,500 km<sup>2</sup> (6,400 sq mi). As a result, populations of 47 threatened species increased, including the critically endangered Yangtze alligator. In the Shanghai area, reestablished wetlands now protect drinking water sources for the city. It is envisaged to extend the network throughout the entire Yangtze to eventually cover 102 areas and 185,000 km<sup>2</sup> (71,000 sq mi). The mayor of <a href="/wiki/Wuhan" title="Wuhan">Wuhan</a> announced that six huge, stagnating urban lakes including the <a href="/wiki/East_Lake_(Wuhan)" title="East Lake (Wuhan)">East Lake (Wuhan)</a> would be reconnected at the cost of US$2.3 billion creating China's largest urban wetland landscape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WWF_UK_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-WWF_UK-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_cities_along_the_river">Major cities along the river</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Major cities along the river">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Populated_places_on_the_Yangtze_River" title="Category:Populated places on the Yangtze River">Category:Populated places on the Yangtze River</a></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Crossings">Crossings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Crossings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Bridges_and_tunnels_across_the_Yangtze_River" title="Bridges and tunnels across the Yangtze River">Bridges and tunnels across the Yangtze River</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yangtze_River_power_line_crossings" title="Yangtze River power line crossings">Yangtze River power line crossings</a></div>
<table class="noprint infobox" id="GeoGroup" style="width: 23em; font-size: 88%; line-height: 1.5em">
<tbody><tr>
<td><b>Map all coordinates in "Yangtze River bridges and tunnels" using:</b> <a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/osm4wiki/cgi-bin/wiki/wiki-osm.pl?project=en&amp;article=Yangtze+River+bridges+and+tunnels">OpenStreetMap</a> 
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>Download coordinates as:</b> <a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/kmlexport?article=Yangtze+River+bridges+and+tunnels">KML</a>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Until 1957, there were no bridges across the Yangtze River from <a href="/wiki/Yibin" title="Yibin">Yibin</a> to Shanghai. For millennia, travelers crossed the river by ferry. On occasions, the crossing may have been dangerous, as evidenced by the <i><a href="/wiki/Zhong%27anlun_Monument" title="Zhong'anlun Monument">Zhong'anlun</a></i> disaster (October 15, 1945).
</p><p>The river stood as a major geographic barrier dividing northern and southern China.  In the first half of the 20th century, rail passengers from Beijing to Guangzhou and Shanghai had to disembark, respectively, at <a href="/wiki/Hanyang_District" title="Hanyang District">Hanyang</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pukou" title="Pukou">Pukou</a>, and cross the river by steam ferry before resuming journeys by train from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wuchang_Railway_Station" title="Wuchang Railway Station">Wuchang</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanjing_West_Railway_Station" title="Nanjing West Railway Station">Nanjing West</a>.
</p><p>After the founding of the People's Republic in 1949, Soviet engineers assisted in the design and construction of the <a href="/wiki/Wuhan_Yangtze_River_Bridge" title="Wuhan Yangtze River Bridge">Wuhan Yangtze River Bridge</a>, a dual-use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_road-rail_bridges" title="List of road-rail bridges">road-rail bridge</a>, built from 1955 to 1957.  It was the first bridge across the Yangtze River.  The second bridge across the river that was built was a single-track railway bridge built upstream in Chongqing in 1959.  The <a href="/wiki/Nanjing_Yangtze_River_Bridge" title="Nanjing Yangtze River Bridge">Nanjing Yangtze River Bridge</a>, also a road-rail bridge, was the first bridge to cross the lower reaches of the Yangtze, in <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a>.  It was built after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sino-Soviet_Split" title="Sino-Soviet Split">Sino-Soviet Split</a> and did not receive foreign assistance.  Road-rail bridges were then built in <a href="/wiki/Zhicheng" title="Zhicheng">Zhicheng</a> (1971) and Chongqing (1980).
</p><p>Bridge-building slowed in the 1980s before resuming in the 1990s and accelerating in the first decade of the 21st century.  The <a href="/wiki/Jiujiang_Yangtze_River_Bridge" title="Jiujiang Yangtze River Bridge">Jiujiang Yangtze River Bridge</a> was built in 1992 as part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jingjiu_railway" title="Jingjiu railway">Beijing-Jiujiang Railway</a>.  A <a href="/wiki/Second_Wuhan_Yangtze_River_Bridge" title="Second Wuhan Yangtze River Bridge">second bridge in Wuhan</a> was completed in 1995.  By 2005, there were a total of 56 bridges and one tunnel across the Yangtze River between Yibin and Shanghai.  These include some of the longest <a href="/wiki/Suspension_bridge" title="Suspension bridge">suspension</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cable-stayed_bridge" title="Cable-stayed bridge">cable-stayed bridges</a> in the world on the Yangtze Delta: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jiangyin_Suspension_Bridge" title="Jiangyin Suspension Bridge">Jiangyin Suspension Bridge</a> (1,385 m, opened in 1999), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Runyang_Bridge" title="Runyang Bridge">Runyang Bridge</a> (1,490 m, opened 2005), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sutong_Bridge" title="Sutong Bridge">Sutong Bridge</a> (1,088 m, opened 2008).  The rapid pace of bridge construction has continued.  The city of Wuhan now has six bridges and one tunnel across the Yangtze.
</p><p>A number of <a href="/wiki/Yangtze_River_power_line_crossings" title="Yangtze River power line crossings">power line crossings</a> have also been built across the river.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="max-width: 1052px;">

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 255px"><div style="width: 255px">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dams">Dams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Dams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Three_Gorges_dam.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Three_Gorges_dam.jpg/220px-Three_Gorges_dam.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Three_Gorges_dam.jpg/330px-Three_Gorges_dam.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Three_Gorges_dam.jpg/440px-Three_Gorges_dam.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Three_Gorges_dam.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Three Gorges Dam in 2006</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yangtze_longitudinal_profile_upstream.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="638" data-file-width="1122" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Yangtze_longitudinal_profile_upstream.JPG/220px-Yangtze_longitudinal_profile_upstream.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Yangtze_longitudinal_profile_upstream.JPG/330px-Yangtze_longitudinal_profile_upstream.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Yangtze_longitudinal_profile_upstream.JPG/440px-Yangtze_longitudinal_profile_upstream.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yangtze_longitudinal_profile_upstream.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram showing dams planned for the upper reaches of the Yangtze River</div></div></div>
<p>As of 2007, there are two dams built on the Yangtze river: <a href="/wiki/Three_Gorges_Dam" title="Three Gorges Dam">Three Gorges Dam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gezhouba_Dam" title="Gezhouba Dam">Gezhouba Dam</a>.
The Three Gorges Dam is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_power_stations" title="List of largest power stations">largest power station in the world</a> by installed capacity, at 22.5 GW.
Several dams are operating or are being constructed on the upper portion of the river, the <a href="/wiki/Jinsha_River" title="Jinsha River">Jinsha River</a>.
Among them, the <a href="/wiki/Xiluodu_Dam" title="Xiluodu Dam">Xiluodu Dam</a> is the third largest power station in the world, and the <a href="/wiki/Baihetan_Dam" title="Baihetan Dam">Baihetan Dam</a>, planned to be commissioned in 2021, will be the second largest after the Three Gorges Dam.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tributaries">Tributaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Yangtze&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Tributaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yangzi_river_ship_yard_on_river_bank.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Yangzi_river_ship_yard_on_river_bank.jpg/220px-Yangzi_river_ship_yard_on_river_bank.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Yangzi_river_ship_yard_on_river_bank.jpg/330px-Yangzi_river_ship_yard_on_river_bank.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Yangzi_river_ship_yard_on_river_bank.jpg/440px-Yangzi_river_ship_yard_on_river_bank.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yangzi_river_ship_yard_on_river_bank.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A shipyard on the banks of the Yangtze building commercial river freight boats</div></div></div>
<p>The Yangtze River has over 700 <a href="/wiki/Tributary" title="Tributary">tributaries</a>. The major tributaries (listed from upstream to downstream) with the locations of where they join the Yangtze are:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Yalong_River" title="Yalong River">Yalong River</a> (<a href="/wiki/Panzhihua" title="Panzhihua">Panzhihua</a>, Sichuan)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Min_River_(Sichuan)" title="Min River (Sichuan)">Min River</a> (<a href="/wiki/Yibin" title="Yibin">Yibin</a>, Sichuan)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tuo_River" title="Tuo River">Tuo River</a> (<a href="/wiki/Luzhou" title="Luzhou">Luzhou</a>, Sichuan)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chishui_River_(Southwest_China)" title="Chishui River (Southwest China)">Chishui River</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hejiang_County" title="Hejiang County">Hejiang</a>, Sichuan)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jialing_River" title="Jialing River">Jialing River</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chongqing" title="Chongqing">Chongqing</a>)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wu_River_(Yangtze_River_tributary)" title="Wu River (Yangtze River tributary)">Wu River</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuling" title="Fuling">Fuling</a>, Chongqing)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Qing_River" title="Qing River">Qing River</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yidu,_Hubei" title="Yidu, Hubei">Yidu</a>, Hubei)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Yuan_River" title="Yuan River">Yuan River</a> (via <a href="/wiki/Dongting_Lake" title="Dongting Lake">Dongting Lake</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lishui_River" title="Lishui River">Lishui River</a> (via <a href="/wiki/Dongting_Lake" title="Dongting Lake">Dongting Lake</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Zi_River" title="Zi River">Zi River</a> (via <a href="/wiki/Dongting_Lake" title="Dongting Lake">Dongting Lake</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Xiang_River" title="Xiang River">Xiang River</a> (<a href="/wiki/Yueyang" title="Yueyang">Yueyang</a>, Hunan)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_River_(Hanshui)" title="Han River (Hanshui)">Han River</a> (<a href="/wiki/Wuhan" title="Wuhan">Wuhan</a>, Hubei)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gan_River" title="Gan River">Gan River</a> (near <a href="/wiki/Jiujiang" title="Jiujiang">Jiujiang</a>, Jiangxi)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Shuiyang_River" title="Shuiyang River">Shuiyang River</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dangtu" title="Dangtu">Dangtu</a>, Anhui)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Qingyi_River_(Anhui)" title="Qingyi River (Anhui)">Qingyi River</a> (<a href="/wiki/Wuhu" title="Wuhu">Wuhu</a>, Anhui)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chao_Lake" title="Chao Lake">Chao Lake</a> water system (<a href="/wiki/Chaohu" title="Chaohu">Chaohu</a>, Anhui)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lake_Tai" title="Lake Tai">Lake Tai</a> water system (Shanghai)</li></ul>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Huai_River" title="Huai River">Huai River</a> flowed into the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Sea" title="Yellow Sea">Yellow Sea</a> until the 20th century, but now primarily discharges into the Yangtze.
</p>
